# COPD/Asthma
Patient presented with dyspnea and chest pain described as 
pressure with associated wheezing. Her constellation of symptoms 
are identical to her previous asthma exacerbations, including 
her chest pain which occur ___ per month and have not been 
escalating in severity or tempo. Per the patient and her family, 
her current presentation is similar to her last admission in 
___ during which time she was treated for an 
asthma/COPD exacerbation. Her triggers for asthma include the 
cold air. Upon evaluation inpatient, she was not wheezing and 
was HDS satting well on room air. She denied wheezing and 
dyspnea during her hospitalization. She was afebrile, without 
infectious symptoms and without a leukocytosis. CXR was clear. 
BNP 1681 on admission however similar to prior levels and 
history and exam do not suggest volume overload. Do not suspect 
her chest pressure or dyspnea symptoms reflect ACS including 
unstable angina. TNT peaked at 0.05 and EKG without ischemic 
changes. She denied worsening in her dyspnea or chest pain 
symptoms and denies dyspnea with exertion. The most likely 
explanation of her constellation of symptoms include poorly 
controlled reactive airway disease in setting of COPD/asthma. 
Because she had a clear lung exam and lack of increasing cough 
or sputum and was asymptomatic on the floor, her presentation 
was not thought to be a true exacerbation. Per her outpatient 
pulmonologist, her outpatient regimen includes albuterol, 
ipratroium and budesonide nebulizers three times daily given 
patient is unable to take deep breaths, has had difficulty with 
taking inhaled corticosteroids or bronchodilators, and thus a 
nebulizer is the best way to deposit her medications. She wad 
discharged with pulmology follow up and cardiology follow up. 
Given her report of chest pressure, it is reasonable to consider 
an outpatient street test to further evaluate. In an effort to 
reduce future hospitalizations, budesonide was increased to 1.0 
mg nebulizer TID.

Peak TNT 0.05. EKG was without evidence of ischemia. Likely

additional fluid resuscitation improved to 130s prior to 
discharge. Likely that a component of hypovolemia was

to hyponatremia and low bicarb raised suspicion for adrenal 
insufficiency. She was noted to have a low AM cortisol level and 
on cosyntropin stimulation test did not show an appropriate 
response (1.3 -> 9.2 at 30 minutes -> 11.4 at 60 minutes). 
Endocrinology was consulted for question of adrenal 
insufficiency and they felt that although she did not have 
cortisol stimulation to 18 at an hour, she demonstrated an 
ability to mount an adrenal response to stress given a >9 point 
rise in her cortisol level. They recommended obtaining an ACTH 
value to help differentiate between primary and secondary 
adrenal insufficiency. They did not advise starting steroids 
given the stabilization of her Na prior to discharge and the 
increased risks of steroid treatment with her age, decreased 
bone density and increased risk for bone fractures. She should 
have a repeat BMP a week post discharge and be referred to 
endocrinology for continued workup and management if she is 
found again to be hyponatremia/hyperkalemic. On discharge her Na 
was 138 and potassium was 4.8.
